<div id="review_128509621" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/128509621" />
    <a title="Jim" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1895570-jim"><img alt="Jim" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1484874117p2/1895570.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/128509621?book_show_action=true">Oct 29, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jim" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jim" href="/user/show/1895570-jim">Jim</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1895570-jim?shelf=travel-classics">travel-classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1895570-jim?shelf=ancient_greece">ancient_greece</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer128509621" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17912354538776953185">This, the last book by its author, is one of a kind. It is merely by chance that, earlier this month, I re-read Herodotus's <b>Histories</b>, so it is so fresh in my mind that I recognized most of the quotes from the 5th century B.C. Greek historian instantly.<br/><br/>But what if I had <b>
  <i>not</i>
</b> read Herodotus recently? Then this would be a rather boring work, an extended commentary on someone with whom I was not familiar. <br/><br/>Kapuscinski and Herodotus shared many traits in common. Both had traveled the world (as the</span>
  <span id="freeText17912354538776953185" style="display:none">This, the last book by its author, is one of a kind. It is merely by chance that, earlier this month, I re-read Herodotus's <b>Histories</b>, so it is so fresh in my mind that I recognized most of the quotes from the 5th century B.C. Greek historian instantly.<br /><br />But what if I had <b>
  <i>not</i>
</b> read Herodotus recently? Then this would be a rather boring work, an extended commentary on someone with whom I was not familiar. <br /><br />Kapuscinski and Herodotus shared many traits in common. Both had traveled the world (as they knew it) from one end to the other. Just by chance, before the Polish journalist left on his first foreign journey -- to India -- a colleague had handed him a copy of the <b>Histories</b> to take with him as reading material. (That unnamed editor was unknowingly his great benefactor.)<br /><br />Over the decades of his long career and across many thousands of miles, the book became Kapuscinski's <i>vade mecum</i>. He referred to it constantly and constantly saw parallels between the Persians and Greeks and all the other peoples involved with them during the upheavals of that time, with the post-colonial turmoils in Africa and Asia of our own time. <br /><br />In the end, reading Herodotus helped Kapuscinski become one of the great travel writers and foreign correspondents of the twentieth century. Toward the end, he concludes:<blockquote>There is no way around this divergence of purpose and means [<b>i.e.</b>, between the objective and subjective viewpoints]. We can try to minimize or mitigate it, but we will never approach the objective ideal. The subjective factor, its deforming presence, will remain impossible to strain out. Herodotus expresses an awareness of this predicament, constantly qualifying what he reports: "as they tell me," "as they maintain," "they present this in various ways," etc. In fact, though, however evolved our methods, we are never in the presence of unmediated history, but of history recounted, presented, history as it appeared to someone, as he or she believes it to have been. This has been the nature of the enterprise always, and the folly may be to believe one can resist it.<br /><br />This fact is perhaps Herodotus's greatest discovery.</blockquote>Earlier, he says:<blockquote>The first to realize the world's essential multiplicity was Herodotus. "We are not alone," he tells Greeks in his opus, and to prove this he undertakes his journeys to the ends of the earth. "We have neighbors, they in turn have their neighbors, and all together we populate a single planet."</blockquote>As one who envies Kapuscinski for his many great books about his travels, and as one who has read Herodotus twice (for apparently good reason, and now I know why), I can see that I could form a similar attachment to the Greek historian.<br /><br />The first time I read Herodotus, I was a Freshman in Ned Nabors's class on Greek Literature in Translation at Dartmouth College. I was in my teens then and dreamed of a life of travel. I have traveled to many places, but something in me keeps saying, "Not enough!" I think, next time, I will take Herodotus with me. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17912354538776953185" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1714442714" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1714442714" />
    <a title="James Murphy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2422285-james-murphy"><img alt="James Murphy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1262206618p2/2422285.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1714442714?book_show_action=true">Oct 09, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James Murphy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James Murphy" href="/user/show/2422285-james-murphy">James Murphy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781400078783" href="/book/show/3328914-travels-with-herodotus">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1714442714" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12399585056979894777">My copy of the book--Vintage International--labels Travels with Herodotus as memoir/history, and it is, but it touched me more as a simple meditation on Herodotus and his The Histories. Kapuscinski is a Polish journalist and traveler who writes here about his 1st trips outside Poland in the mid-20th century. He was given a copy of Herodotus as a companion on his 1st assignment abroad. It touched him deeply, as his own book touched me.<br/><br/>Kapuscincki uses The Histories as a way to gauge his own sense</span>
  <span id="freeText12399585056979894777" style="display:none">My copy of the book--Vintage International--labels Travels with Herodotus as memoir/history, and it is, but it touched me more as a simple meditation on Herodotus and his The Histories. Kapuscinski is a Polish journalist and traveler who writes here about his 1st trips outside Poland in the mid-20th century. He was given a copy of Herodotus as a companion on his 1st assignment abroad. It touched him deeply, as his own book touched me.<br /><br />Kapuscincki uses The Histories as a way to gauge his own sense of wonder as he travels, as his own sense of being an other in strange societies. He tries to see his own reactions to strangeness in light of the way he thinks Herodotus reacted. In seeing himself intently observing the places to which he travels--India, China, Iran, much of Africa--he uses Herodotus as a guide in how to see and understand, even though Herodotus traveled and wrote without maps and without a large collection of written resources. Kapuscinski constantly reminds us how deep in history Herodotus lived and chronicled his travels--he writes that the Persian armies described in The Histories existed 24 centuries before Napoleon, that the Middle East written about was one a thousand years before the arrival of Islam--yet still finds ways to see their experiences as similar.<br /><br />One of the elements he learned from The Histories was contrast. The ancient world in which Herodotus traveled could easily be seen as divided into east-west, Asia-Europe, Persia-Greece. Kapuscinski observes contrasts of his own. Besides his own obvious east-west, that of the Cold War, he compares the open character of India's population with the closed nature of the Chinese. In Algeria he sees 2 Islams, those of river and desert, or the new vs the traditional. The book itself is halved between Kapuscinski's travels and Herodotus's.<br /><br />The final chapter is thoughts on history, which he sees, like Herodotus, as created by people through the continuous flow of their everday lives. And the history created today is mirrored by the history Herodotus found because human nature is a constant. This chapter on the meaning and uses of history is terrific.<br /><br />Thanks to my Goodreads friend Lyn Elliott whose review of Travels with Herodotus was the inspiration for my coming to the book. Kapuscinski writes late in his book that Herodotus was a discovery for him but that he came to think of him as his Herodotus. Similarly, I now know I have Kapuscinski and that this is only my 1st of his books.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12399585056979894777" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Beth Bonini" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Beth Bonini" href="/user/show/3106785-beth-bonini">Beth Bonini</a>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3106785-beth-bonini?shelf=historical">historical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3106785-beth-bonini?shelf=travel">travel</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1293888730" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1624379841168907638">Years ago, I remember reading The English Patient and becoming aware, for the first time, of Herodotus. In that book, the character of Katharine Clifton was reading Herodotus's Histories. I've still not read Herotodus, but after having read this book -- which is full of references and quoted text from what Kapuscinski describes as "world literature's first great work of reportage"-- I fully understand why it was such a apt choice of reading material for an English woman trapped in Cairo during W</span>
  <span id="freeText1624379841168907638" style="display:none">Years ago, I remember reading The English Patient and becoming aware, for the first time, of Herodotus. In that book, the character of Katharine Clifton was reading Herodotus's Histories. I've still not read Herotodus, but after having read this book -- which is full of references and quoted text from what Kapuscinski describes as "world literature's first great work of reportage"-- I fully understand why it was such a apt choice of reading material for an English woman trapped in Cairo during WWII. Herodotus writes about the constant state of war in the ancient world, of power-mad rulers and the clash between East and West, Muslim and Christian. It must have put the 20th century cataclysm into some sort of context. As bad as it was, it was nothing the world hadn't seen before. There's always some megalomaniac determined on conquering other people and their resources.<br /><br />This is a far-ranging book in which the Polish journalist travels the world and learns his trade -- all the time musing on the lessons of Herodotus (how to observe people with interest and curiosity, and then to report objectively on those observations). The book begins with his impoverished childhood in post-war Communist Poland and then follows his travels in a rather rambling fashion -- from India to the Middle East to Africa. Much of the book takes place in the post-colonial period, as those ancient countries are throwing off their European rulers and struggling to redefine themselves. All the while, Kapuscinski -- cut off from home, family, native language -- cleaves to Herodotus as a fellow traveller attempting to make sense of the world. <br /><br />I doubt that I will ever bother to read Herodotus in the original, and I certainly won't go to most (if not all) the countries that Kapuscinski visits in this book, but as an "armchair traveller" I enjoyed my exposure to far-off lands.<br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1624379841168907638" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_42847010" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lisa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lisa" href="/user/show/1901026-lisa">Lisa</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer11211580796190983128">The book I wanted this to be was one where, upon reading something from Herodotus such as "According to the stories of the Trachis, the left bank of the Ister is populated by bees," the author is going to place this anecdote on a modern map, go there, write about what became of the Trachis and who they are now, and where this business with bees might have come from.<br/><br/>That is not this book. There is one bit where Kapuściński visits a site from The Histories, Persepolis, but he doesn't see it all be</span>
  <span id="freeText11211580796190983128" style="display:none">The book I wanted this to be was one where, upon reading something from Herodotus such as "According to the stories of the Trachis, the left bank of the Ister is populated by bees," the author is going to place this anecdote on a modern map, go there, write about what became of the Trachis and who they are now, and where this business with bees might have come from.<br /><br />That is not this book. There is one bit where Kapuściński visits a site from The Histories, Persepolis, but he doesn't see it all because it's hot and he gets tired.<br /><br />Perhaps Kapuściński wanted to write a book about Herodotus, and he wanted to write a memoir, but he got hot and tired and decided to combine them into one book. <br /><br />The tie-in is, both Kapuściński and Herodotus are journalists traveling the world. <br /><br />The memoir bits of the book are fragmentary. He was in the Congo during a civil war, an in Iran during the revolution. Pretty exciting stuff, no? He barely mentions anything about it.<br /><br />The Herodotus bits do have some interesting ruminations on history, human nature and such. But more often he simply retells stories from The Histories, quotes from them extensively, and then asks a series of rhetorical questions. Like twenty in a row. Turns out, that makes me irrationally irritated.<br /><br />The insightful thoughts on Herodotus are tempered by ones such as Herodotus "is not bothered by the lack of telephone or the airplane, nor does he worry about not having a bicycle." Deep, dude.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer4636254444589274154">One of my biggest regrets in life so far is that I never got to take Ryszard Kapuscinski out to dinner. <div id="review_2734305177" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2734305177" />
    <a title="Murtaza " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13716226-murtaza"><img alt="Murtaza " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1531516448p2/13716226.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2734305177?book_show_action=true">Mar 02, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Murtaza " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Murtaza " href="/user/show/13716226-murtaza">Murtaza </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2734305177" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11586416182586560176">Ryszard Kapuściński was one of the strangest yet most charming journalists of the past century. His books are weirdly dreamlike. A foreign correspondent for a Polish newspaper, his writings sometimes feel as though he is passing through great events in a philosophical haze. Despite that he is still able to provide lucid and captivating accounts of what he sees. If a journalist could be accused of being a magical realist, it would be Kapuściński. His dispatches were like the accounts of grand myt</span>
  <span id="freeText11586416182586560176" style="display:none">Ryszard Kapuściński was one of the strangest yet most charming journalists of the past century. His books are weirdly dreamlike. A foreign correspondent for a Polish newspaper, his writings sometimes feel as though he is passing through great events in a philosophical haze. Despite that he is still able to provide lucid and captivating accounts of what he sees. If a journalist could be accused of being a magical realist, it would be Kapuściński. His dispatches were like the accounts of grand myths.<br /><br />This book is an account of his travels around the world while reading Herodotus' <i>The Histories</i>. He passes through entire countries within a few pages, without always describing much of what he saw. The book is mostly psychological reflections, interspersed with his own excerpts and commentaries on <i>The Histories</i>. Kapuściński's reflections on what he learned from Herodotus as a journalist and traveler and what the ancient Greek historian meant to him are moving. Anyone who seeks knowledge from travel and reading will recognize a kindred spirit in Kapuściński, just as he recognized one in Herodotus.<br /><br />Conscious that he also came from an impoverished country, Kapuściński was less judgmental and imperious than some European and American journalists. He comes across as a curious, guileless and earnest observer of the places he visits. It makes his books rather easygoing and pleasant. There is no undertone of aggression.<br /><br />Kapuściński was accused later in life of playing a bit too loose with the facts for a journalist. I'm not surprised by this, given the cerebral and somewhat dreamy nature of his writing. Nonetheless I don't think that this detracts from the value of his work, as long as one is cognizant. I feel that in this book he was trying to explain the limits of human knowledge by exploring the nature of his own work and the work of as great a historian as Herodotus. The writing and translation from Polish are beautiful. Highly recommended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11586416182586560176" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1771594434" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1771594434" />
    <a title="Dan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14090325-dan"><img alt="Dan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1365317812p2/14090325.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1771594434?book_show_action=true">Sep 29, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dan" href="/user/show/14090325-dan">Dan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1771594434" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9720640242208610506">Man, what a book. I picked this up thinking it would be a simple travelogue but it's much, much more. It would have been interesting enough if it was just a straightforward retelling of Kapuscinski's travels through India, China and Africa during the 50s and 60s as a foreign correspondent for a Polish newspaper, but what he does by relating his journey through passages in Herodotus' the Histories puts it on a whole other level. During his travels, he always goes back to his trusty Histories, usi</span>
  <span id="freeText9720640242208610506" style="display:none">Man, what a book. I picked this up thinking it would be a simple travelogue but it's much, much more. It would have been interesting enough if it was just a straightforward retelling of Kapuscinski's travels through India, China and Africa during the 50s and 60s as a foreign correspondent for a Polish newspaper, but what he does by relating his journey through passages in Herodotus' the Histories puts it on a whole other level. During his travels, he always goes back to his trusty Histories, using his examinations into the mind and motives of Herodotus to put what he's doing as a journalist in perspective. It's inspiring in a way, because there are still people like Herodotus today with an inquisitive nature who want to better understand the world around them and explain it, to learn stories and amass knowledge so it will be there for others, but haunting at the same time because the very nature of how Herodutus relates what he finds - "I am told," "As best they could remember," shows how subjective history is, and how much is forgotten and lost, whole empires, lives, deaths, events, battles gone, never to return. All that's left after the inexorable march of history is ruins, shards of amphorae at the bottom of the sea, third and fourth hand retellings and so on. There's so much in this short book that I really can't do it justice here, meditations on the nature of place, on fate, on human nature, it really blew me away. Can't wait to read more by him as well as The Histories itself. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9720640242208610506" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5237420-ana?shelf=a-little-historical">a-little-historical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5237420-ana?shelf=about-murders">about-murders</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1462291417" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer207884153915297291">I'm not the one to deny my high school history teacher's taste in literature, and when he recommends an author to me, I pretty much make sure that I read that author as soon as I can. I not only know that it will be a good read from a literary point of view, but that it will force my analytical side as well and teach me a thing or two. As Kapuscinski did. <br/><br/>Reading history books can get a bit tedious, as times. You get sucked in this whirlwind of names, dates and pointless statistical information,</span>
  <span id="freeText207884153915297291" style="display:none">I'm not the one to deny my high school history teacher's taste in literature, and when he recommends an author to me, I pretty much make sure that I read that author as soon as I can. I not only know that it will be a good read from a literary point of view, but that it will force my analytical side as well and teach me a thing or two. As Kapuscinski did. <br /><br />Reading history books can get a bit tedious, as times. You get sucked in this whirlwind of names, dates and pointless statistical information, all of them in an effort to focus the reader on (frankly) boring stuff. Or at least, this reader, yours truly. I enjoy humanity, and Kapuscinski definitely wrote with that in mind. <br /><br />This work is an intertwined version of a travel journal, between the author's walks of life and the mighty Herodotus' roads as well. Some two thousans years between them, and it is still easy to see how no matter how far away or how far apart, people will be people nevertheless the circumstance. For someone who enjoys reading about the nitty-gritty actions and ideas of people from everywhere, this comparison between the travels of an "ancien regime" (so to say) historian that lived farther from me than Jesus himself (if you believe in His existence, which I don't), and the travels of a(n almost) contemporary is a truly captivating one. <br /><br />I recommend this work to anyone with a sweet tooth for history ... and for anyone with a sweet tooth for culture, humanity and common-sense thought, as well. </span>
  <a data-text-id="207884153915297291" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_184192913" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Kristianne" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/844730-kristianne"><img alt="Kristianne" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1472341034p2/844730.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/184192913?book_show_action=true">Jul 11, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kristianne" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kristianne" href="/user/show/844730-kristianne">Kristianne</a>

          added it




        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/844730-kristianne?shelf=places">places</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer184192913" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9164399699316986601"><br/>Reading Kapuscinski while bed-bound with illness is a blessing and a curse. It offers escape and yet makes my infirmity more pronounced. The vibrancy of the places the author visits and the experiences he has in each is intoxicating. I wanted to pack up my meager belongings and set out again for a new adventure. Exploring the world is intoxicating and addictive. Kapuscinski writes in the book:<br/>“A journey, after all, neither begins in the instant we set out, nor ends when we have reached our doors</span>
  <span id="freeText9164399699316986601" style="display:none"><br />Reading Kapuscinski while bed-bound with illness is a blessing and a curse. It offers escape and yet makes my infirmity more pronounced. The vibrancy of the places the author visits and the experiences he has in each is intoxicating. I wanted to pack up my meager belongings and set out again for a new adventure. Exploring the world is intoxicating and addictive. Kapuscinski writes in the book:<br />“A journey, after all, neither begins in the instant we set out, nor ends when we have reached our doorstep once again. It starts much earlier and is really never over, because the film of memory continues running on inside us long after we have come to a physical standstill. Indeed, there exists something like a contagion of travel, and the disease is essentially incurable.” P79<br />My inability to do so much as leave my bed for more than two hours at a time of course made jumping on a plane inconceivable, so I read about Kapuscinski’s travels instead. From India to China and the Congo, I was caught up in his experiences. It isn’t as though he lured me with outrageous adventure; in fact, Kapuscinski’s travel writing is distinctly focused on the internal experience of travel. He tells us about the world outside, but those foreign events and places share equal billing with the internal space he explores and the meditations on ancient Greek history as he reads and rereads Herodotus. He says of the book:<br />“…one must read Herodotus’s book—and every great book—repeatedly; with each reading it will reveal another layer, previously overlooked themes, images, and meanings. For within every great book there are several others.” P219<br />Kapuscinski’s blend of active involvement in the world, interior medication and educational consideration of Herodotus’ classic book made it the perfect arm chair (or, in my case, mosquito netted bed) travel experience.<br /><br />“We do not really know what draws a human being out into the world. Is it curiosity? A hunger for experience? An addiction to wonderment? The man who ceases to be astonished is hollow, possessed of an extinguished heart. If he believes that everything has already happened, that he has seen it all, then something most precious has died within him—the delight in life.” P269<br /><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9164399699316986601" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

<span id="freeTextContainer13895229163231211559">Enjoyable, but slightly disappointing in that I expected it to be a bit better. This is partially autobiography and partially a poem in praise of Herodotus. (And I will go back and read Herodotus as a result). Kapucinski is really comparing his life to that of Herodotus, and the comparison is not unfair. <br/><br/>The first chapter is marvellous - in a few pages capturing post war Poland, the feeling of living under communism, and the sense of being at that age when one is deciding what to do with ones li</span>
  <span id="freeText13895229163231211559" style="display:none">Enjoyable, but slightly disappointing in that I expected it to be a bit better. This is partially autobiography and partially a poem in praise of Herodotus. (And I will go back and read Herodotus as a result). Kapucinski is really comparing his life to that of Herodotus, and the comparison is not unfair. <br /><br />The first chapter is marvellous - in a few pages capturing post war Poland, the feeling of living under communism, and the sense of being at that age when one is deciding what to do with ones life. The rest of the book is good, but not as good. One literary device he uses is to ponder questions about how Herodotus lived, thought and wrote by raising questions. I just thought he does this too much and I found it a little irritating after a while. <br /><br />Perhaps my review is unfair because I had such high expectations. I first read Kapuscinski as a teenager and his books developed a passion in other cultures and in travel. Ironically, when I first read him I did not even know he was Polish - a place I now plan to live part of the time, but in those days a far off land behind the iron curtain. <br /><br />I did not find this as good as some of his pure travel/journalistic books. However, its a worthy read and you may well enjoy it more than me. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13895229163231211559" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1008340588" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1008340588" />
    <a title="Ian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/31384949-ian"><img alt="Ian" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1584889764p2/31384949.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1008340588?book_show_action=true">Jul 27, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ian" href="/user/show/31384949-ian">Ian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31384949-ian?shelf=travel">travel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31384949-ian?shelf=reportage">reportage</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1008340588" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17757292511190609296">There is an old cliché that "a picture is worth a thousand words" and it's very often true, but not when the words are set down by the late Ryszard Kapuściński, a writer and reporter capable of conjuring up images that stay with the reader for decades. A number of his previous books, "Another Day of Life", "The Emperor", "The Soccer War", "Shah of Shahs", and "The Shadow of the Sun", are amongst my all time favourites. This book is however different in feel from those mentioned above, focusing m</span>
  <span id="freeText17757292511190609296" style="display:none">There is an old cliché that "a picture is worth a thousand words" and it's very often true, but not when the words are set down by the late Ryszard Kapuściński, a writer and reporter capable of conjuring up images that stay with the reader for decades. A number of his previous books, "Another Day of Life", "The Emperor", "The Soccer War", "Shah of Shahs", and "The Shadow of the Sun", are amongst my all time favourites. This book is however different in feel from those mentioned above, focusing mainly on the author's admiration for the Ancient Greek writer Herodotus, author of "The Histories" and considered to be the first person ever to set down a description of the World (as he knew it) and its past. In this book Kapuściński gives us a rather random description of some of his travels across the World, interspersed with how he learned much of his reporter's style from Herodotus, how the Greek inspired him to regard other cultures non-judgementally, and how Kapuściński was enthralled by Herodotus' description of the Persian Empire and the Greco - Persian Wars of the fifth century BC. <br /><br />The early part of this book is up to the same standard as other Kapuściński books. The author gives us an eye-popping description of his first foreign posting at the age of 26 - to India, a trip for which he seems to have been given no instructions and very little money, and for which he initially had almost no English. Flying via Rome, he describes himself arriving in Italy sporting fashion "à la Warsaw Pact 1956" but this light relief for the reader is mixed with appalled fascination as Kapuściński describes the scene on his arrival at Calcutta's main railway station. The following year he is sent to China, a journey he makes by flying from Warsaw to Hong Kong via Amsterdam and Tokyo; catching a train to the Chinese border, and crossing the border on foot before walking a mile with suitcase in hand to catch a train to Beijing - a reminder perhaps of how much easier international travel has become in the last 60 years. However, as the book progresses more and more of the text is given over to extended descriptions of "The Histories". Whilst it's true that I now know much more about Herodotus than I used to, I personally would have preferred to read more of Kapuściński's own journeys, and it's for this reason that I haven't rated this book quite as highly as others I have read by the same author. Still an enjoyable book, but if you have not read Kapuściński before, read his other work first.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17757292511190609296" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/42107369" />
    <a title="George" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1414224-george"><img alt="George" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1248471697p2/1414224.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/42107369?book_show_action=true">Jan 06, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="George" class="user" itemprop="url" name="George" href="/user/show/1414224-george">George</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781400078783" href="/book/show/3328914-travels-with-herodotus">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer42107369" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6350231536041966391">From the title page: “Ryszard Kapuscinski, Poland’s most celebrated foreign correspondent, was born in 1932 in Pinsk (in what is now Belarus) and spent four decades reporting on Asia, Latin America, and Africa. He died in 2007.” This remarkable book is the account of Kapuscinski’s years in the field, traveling for the first time beyond the Iron Curtain to India, China, Ethiopia, and other interesting locales. But, it is more than an interesting travelogue. He travels with a copy of Herodotus’s “</span>
  <span id="freeText6350231536041966391" style="display:none">From the title page: “Ryszard Kapuscinski, Poland’s most celebrated foreign correspondent, was born in 1932 in Pinsk (in what is now Belarus) and spent four decades reporting on Asia, Latin America, and Africa. He died in 2007.” This remarkable book is the account of Kapuscinski’s years in the field, traveling for the first time beyond the Iron Curtain to India, China, Ethiopia, and other interesting locales. But, it is more than an interesting travelogue. He travels with a copy of Herodotus’s “The Histories,” and describes how the words of the Greek historian helped shape his own view of the world. Over 2,000 years ago, Herodotus traveled far and wide throughout the world of his time, and recorded his insights, analyses, and evaluations of that world; his discovery – “that the cultures of others are a mirror in which we can examine ourselves in order to understand ourselves better.” As Kapuscinski follows in the Greek’s footsteps, he relates how Herodotus influenced his own insights and evaluations. The book is filled with interesting stories and excerpts from “The Histories,” which tempt me to read Herodotus myself. The English translation is outstanding. I highly recommend this book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6350231536041966391" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer10726174400029218206">A whirlwind tour of the world that finds Ryszard Kapucinski, considered the greatest foreign correspondent of the twentieth century, in India, China, the Congo, Kenya, and elsewhere during revolutions, civil wars, and social tumults of the post-WWII period.   <span id="freeText10726174400029218206" style="display:none">A whirlwind tour of the world that finds Ryszard Kapucinski, considered the greatest foreign correspondent of the twentieth century, in India, China, the Congo, Kenya, and elsewhere during revolutions, civil wars, and social tumults of the post-WWII period. Kapucinski's travel companion is the great Herodotus, himself considered the "Father of History." The writer goes back and forth between reportage, observation, social criticism, and Herodotus' own ideas preserved in his "Histories." At times the narrative is interrupted and important context not delivered -- I think, strangely enough, that this book would have been better structured with Herodotus in the background rather than foreground as it often gets tedious to read his words when Kapucinski's writing is what keeps the narrative moving. </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('n97Cv8qRO1j2w93aRK4sY+i3775GAi7oalgxo3LyRpbGsvmCk3yznHHGu0jT2eCf/meakwGA7YsXQDWlp29CVg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('6rk+bzXeAImUcgZSl74AheITfwRLe3JNDd/Wnw48tLSz1QVSbDOITRN3YMAAycx59MMKKQz5sS5wx9KZ26GwdA==')})
  }

But though they are new to modern statehood, this is a region rich in ancient history, culture, and landscapes unlike anywhere else in the world.\n\n\nTraveling alone, Erika Fatland is a true adventurer in every sense. In Sovietistan, she takes the reader on a compassionate and insightful journey to explore how their Soviet heritage has influenced these countries, with governments experimenting with both democracy and dictatorships.\n\n\nIn Kyrgyzstani villages, she meets victims of the tradition of bride snatching; she visits the huge and desolate Polygon in Kazakhstan where the Soviet Union tested explosions of nuclear bombs; she meets shrimp gatherers on the banks of the dried out Aral Sea; she witnesses the fall of a dictator.\n\n\nShe travels incognito through Turkmenistan, a country that is closed to journalists. She meets exhausted human rights activists in Kazakhstan, survivors from the massacre in Osh in 2010, and German Mennonites that found paradise on the Kyrgyzstani plains 200 years ago. We learn how ancient customs clash with gas production and witness the underlying conflicts between ethnic Russians and the majority in a country that is slowly building its future in nationalist colors.\n\n\nOnce the frontier of the Soviet Union, life follows another pace of time. Amidst the treasures of Samarkand and the brutalist Soviet architecture, Sovietistan is a rare and unforgettable adventure.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"15188890942109647153\" href=\"#\" Rozmowa, jaką przeprowadziła z nim autorka, stanowi kanwę książki będącej relacją z okresu likwidacji getta warszawskiego i powstania w 1943 roku. Krall powiedziała, że Marek Edelman zawsze miał ambicję zdążyć – jako żołnierz podczas wojny i jako lekarz w czasach powojennych. Chciał być punktualnie na miejscu wtedy, gdy zjawiała się śmierć, żeby ją, jeśli to tylko możliwe, uprzedzić. Edelman „ma swoje sprawy z Panem Bogiem, mam wrażenie, że i Pan Bóg ma z nim swoje sprawy”, stwierdziła po latach pisarka wjednym zwywiadów. \n\nOd 1997 roku książka cieszy się niesłabnącym zainteresowaniem, doczekała się tłumaczeń na wiele języków, a w Polsce sprzedała się już w nakładach ponad 170 tysięcy egzemplarzy.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"12775173480196730354\" href=\"#\" Ma anch<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText587473689587392509\" style=\"display:none\">La sfida col Minotauro nel labirinto, la testa tagliata di Medusa, l\'impresa degli Argonauti, Orfeo che scende nell\'Aldilà per salvare la donna amata: i miti eroici dell\'antica Grecia sono un caleidoscopio di racconti infiniti e stupefacenti. Ma anche un repertorio di favole orrende e bizzarre. Medea che uccide i suoi stessi figli. Edipo che ammazza il padre e poi si unisce con la madre. Eracle che abbandona le sue fatiche per ingozzarsi di arrosti e di dolci. Teseo che, invece di salvare le fanciulle, le stupra. L\'eroe greco aveva molti lati oscuri. Era una creatura smisurata, un essere prodigioso, eccessivo nel suo furore e nelle sue passioni. E non sempre era arruolato al servizio del Bene: stava piuttosto oltre il Bene e il Male, oltre tutte le regole e le leggi dell\'umano. Era venerato come lo sono oggi i nostri santi patroni. Si raccoglievano le sue reliquie, si portavano offerte sulla sua tomba. Ma soprattutto ci si divertiva a narrare le sue imprese in forme sempre diverse e sempre nuove. Nei miti eroici si trova la matrice di tutte le narrazioni future. Ci sono amori, avventure, intrighi, prodigi, mostri. Ci sono viaggi fino alla fine del mondo e love-stories che sfidano anche la morte. Tutto quello che ancora oggi ci appassiona e ci commuove quando leggiamo un romanzo o guardiamo un film, i greci lo avevano già narrato nei loro miti.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"587473689587392509\" <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer13296900202495893604">Ryszard Kapuściński debuted as a poet in <i>Dziś i jutro</i> at the age of 17 and has been a journalist, writer, and publicist. In 1964 he was appointed to the Polish Press Agency and began traveling around the developing world and reporting on wars, coups and revolutions in Asia, the Americas, and Europe; he lived through twenty-seven revolutions and coups, was jailed forty times, and survived four deat</span>
  <span id="freeText13296900202495893604" style="display:none">Ryszard Kapuściński debuted as a poet in <i>Dziś i jutro</i> at the age of 17 and has been a journalist, writer, and publicist. In 1964 he was appointed to the Polish Press Agency and began traveling around the developing world and reporting on wars, coups and revolutions in Asia, the Americas, and Europe; he lived through twenty-seven revolutions and coups, was jailed forty times, and survived four death sentences. During some of this time he also worked for the Polish Secret Service, although little is known of his role.<br /><br />See also <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/award/show/9041-ryszard-kapuci-ski-prize" rel="nofollow noopener">Ryszard Kapuściński Prize</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="13296900202495893604" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/1498146-podr-e-z-herodotem">Quotes from Travels with Hero...</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;A journey, after all, neither begins in the instant we set out, nor ends when we have reached our door step once again. It starts much earlier and is really never over, because the film of memory continues running on inside of us long after we have come to a physical standstill. Indeed, there exists something like a contagion of travel, and the disease is essentially incurable.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/quotes/152311-a-journey-after-all-neither-begins-in-the-instant-we">206 likes</a>
            </nobr>
            <div class="clear"></div>
          </div>
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;There aren't many such enthusiasts born. The average person is not especially curious about the world. He is alive, and being somehow obliged to deal with this condition, feels the less effort it requires, the better. Whereas learning about the world is labor, and a great all-consuming one at that. Most people develop quite antithetical talents, in fact - to look without seeing, to listen without hearing, mainly to preserve onself within oneself.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
